style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eCreate or Update Asset Inventory\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eFoundational to the success of any threat hunt is an asset inventory that includes devices not covered by the standard security tool stack, such as edge devices and other appliances. Because these appliances lack support for traditional security tools an inventory is critical for developing effective compensating controls and detections. Especially important is to track the management interface addresses of these appliances, as they act as the default gateway that malware and threat actor commands will egress out of.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant recommends organizations take a multi-step approach to building or updating this inventory:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eKnown knowns: \u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eBegin with the appliance classes that all organizations use: firewalls, VPN concentrators, virtualization platforms, conferencing systems, badging, and file storage.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eKnown unknowns: \u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eWork across teams to brainstorm appliance classes that may be more specialized to your organization, but the security organization likely lacks visibility into.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eUnknown unknowns: \u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThese are the appliances that were supposed to be decommissioned but weren’t, sales POVs, and others. Consider using network visibility tools or your existing EDR to scan for “live” IP addresses that do not show in your EDR reports. This has the added benefit of identifying unmanaged devices that should have EDR but don’t.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 2: Asset inventory\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/brickstorm-iceberg.max-1700x1700.jpg","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/brickstorm-iceberg.max-1100x1100.jpg 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/brickstorm-iceberg.max-1700x1700.jpg 1667w"," 1060px,  1667px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/brickstorm-iceberg.max-1700x1700.jpg"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch4\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eFile and Backup Scan for BRICKSTORM\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eYARA rules have proven to be the most effective method for detecting BRICKSTORM binaries on appliances. We are sharing relevant YARA rules in the appendix section of this post. Yara can be difficult to run at scale, but some backup solutions provide the ability to run YARA across the backup data store. Mandiant is aware of multiple customers who have identified BRICKSTORM through this method. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eTo aid organizations in hunting for BRICKSTORM activity in their environments, \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/mandiant/brickstorm-scanner\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant released a scanner script\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e, which can run on appliances and other Linux or BSD-based systems. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eInternet Traffic from Edge Devices and Appliances\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eUse the inventory of appliance management IP addresses to hunt for evidence of malware beaconing in network logs. In general, appliances should not communicate with the public Internet from management IP addresses except to download updates and send crash analytics to the manufacturer. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eEstablished outbound traffic to domains or IP addresses not controlled by the appliance manufacturer should be regarded as very suspicious and warranting forensic review of the appliance. BRICKSTORM can use DNS over HTTP (DoH), which should be similarly rare when sourced from appliance management IP addresses.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAccess to Windows Systems from Appliances\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe threat actor primarily accessed Windows machines (both desktops and servers) using type 3 (network) logins, although in some cases the actor also established RDP sessions. Appliances should rarely log in to Windows desktops or servers and any connections should be treated as suspicious. Some examples of false positives could include VPN appliances using a known service account to connect to a domain controller in order to perform LDAP lookups and authenticated vulnerability scanners using a well-known service account. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eIn addition to EDR telemetry, Terminal Services logs and Security event logs, defenders should obtain and parse the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-server/administration/user-access-logging/get-started-with-user-access-logging\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eWindows User Access Log (UAL)\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e. The UAL is stored on Windows Servers inside the directory \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eWindows\\System32\\LogFiles\\Sum\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e and can be parsed using open-source tools such as \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://ericzimmerman.github.io/#!index.md\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eSumECmd\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e. This log source records attempted authenticated connections to Windows systems and often retains artifacts going back much longer than typical Windows event logs. Note that this log source includes successful and unsuccessful logins, but is still useful to identify suspicious activity sourced from appliances.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAccess to Credentials and Secrets\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eUse the forensic capabilities of EDR tools to acquire \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://medium.com/ce-digital-forensics/shellbag-analysis-18c9b2e87ac7\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eWindows Shellbags\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e artifacts from Windows workstations and servers. Shellbags records folder paths that are browsed by a user with the Windows Explorer application. Use an \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/williballenthin/shellbags\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eopen-source parser\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e to extract the relevant data and look for patterns of activity that are suspicious:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAccess to folder paths where the initiating user is a service account, especially service accounts that are unfamiliar or rarely used\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eFile browsing activity sourced from servers that include a Windows Universal Naming Convention (UNC) path that points to a workstation (e.g., \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\\\\bobwin7.corp.local\\browsing\\path)\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eFile browsing activity to folder paths that contain credential data, such as:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:circle;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eBrowser profile paths (e.g., \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e%appdata%\\Mozilla\\Firefox\\Profiles\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e)\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:circle;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAppdata locations used to store session tokens (e.g., \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eUsers\\\u0026lt;username\u0026gt;\\.azure\\\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e)\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:circle;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eWindows credential vault (\u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e%appdatalocal%\\Microsoft\\Credentials\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e)\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:circle;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eData Protection API (DPAPI) keys (\u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e%appdata%\\Microsoft\\Protect\\\u0026lt;SID\u0026gt;\\\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e)\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAccess to M365 Mailboxes using Enterprise Application\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant has observed this actor use common techniques to conduct bulk email access and exfiltration from Microsoft 365 Exchange Online. Organizations should follow our guidance outlined in our APT29 \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://services.google.com/fh/files/misc/remediation-and-hardening-strategies-for-microsoft-wp-en.pdf\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ewhitepaper\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e to hunt for these techniques. Although the white paper specifically references APT29, these techniques have become widely used by many groups. In multiple investigations the threat actor used a Microsoft Entra ID Enterprise Application with \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003email.read\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e or \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003efull_access_as_app\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e scopes to access mailboxes of key individuals in the victim organization.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eTo hunt for this activity, we recommend a phased approach:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eEnumerate the Enterprise Applications and Application Registrations with graph permissions that can read all mail.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eFor each application, validate that there is at least one secret or certificate configured for it. Record the Application (client) ID\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eConduct a free text search against the Unified Audit Log or the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eOfficeActivity\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e table in Sentinel for the client IDs from step 2. This will return the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003emailitemsaccessed\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e events that recorded the application accessing mail.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eFor each application analyze the source IP addresses and user-agent strings for discrepancies. Legitimate usage of the applications should occur from well-defined IP addresses. Additionally, look for focused interest in key personnel mailboxes across multiple days.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eWhen accessing M365 and other internet-facing services the actor has used multiple commercial VPN and proxy providers. Mandiant has found evidence of the threat actor using PIA, NordVPN, Surfshark, VPN Unlimited, and PrivadoVPN, although there is no reason for these to be the only solutions used. There is also evidence to support that this actor has access to a purpose-built obfuscation network built from compromised small office/home office routers. Mandiant has no knowledge of how these routers are being compromised. The exit nodes for commercial VPNs and obfuscation networks change rapidly and sharing atomic indicators for hunting purposes is unlikely to yield results. Instead, identify the key individuals in the organization, with respect to the organization vertical and likely goals of the threat actor. Fetch \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003emailitemsaccessed\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e logs for those mailboxes for the last year or as long as retention allows. Analyze the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eSessionID\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e values of the log events and look for IDs that span multiple IP addresses where the IP addresses are not in the user’s typical geographic location.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eCloning of Sensitive Virtual Machines\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eOn VMware vCenter servers, VPXD logs contain valuable information for VM management related tasks such as clone events, powering on and off a VM, and creating snapshots. The threat actor often used the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eVSPHERE.LOCAL\\Administrator\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e account when cloning VMs and targeted VMs that would contain credentials such as password vaults and domain controllers. The threat actor would delete the cloned VM shortly after cloning, and primarily operated between the hours of 01:00 and 10:00 UTC. Investigators should search vCenter VPXD logs for activity that matches the aforementioned criteria and confirm if the cloning activity was intended or not.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eCreation of Local vCenter and ESXi Accounts\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant identified evidence the threat actor created a new local account to install BRICKSTORM and then removed the account after they were done. The following logs show the threat actor using the local Administrator account to create a new local account and add it to the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eBashShellAdministrators\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e group. The threat actor established an SSH connection from a compromised appliance to vCenter using the newly created account and installed the BRICKSTORM backdoor on vCenter. Shortly after, the threat actor deleted the account. Investigators should review audit logs in \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e/var/log/audit/sso-events/audit_events.log\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e for the creation and deletion of local accounts and validate their purpose. In one instance, the threat actor named the account with a similar naming convention as a local service account used for backups on vCenter.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e2025-04-01T06:45:32 {\u0026quot;user\u0026quot;:\u0026quot;Administrator@VSPHERE.LOCAL\u0026quot;,\u0026quot;client\u0026quot;:\u0026quot;\u0026quot;,\u0026quot;timestamp\u0026quot;:\u0026quot;04/01/2025 06:45:32 GMT\u0026quot;,\u0026quot;description\u0026quot;:\u0026quot;Creating local person user \u0026#39;\u0026lt;account_name\u0026gt;\u0026#39; In most organizations it is relatively rare for legitimate direct access to the ESXi hosts over SSH. In many cases the SSH server is disabled by default. Write rules to alert on log events when SSH is enabled for a vSphere platform appliance.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eRogue VMs\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eOrganizations should review VMWare audit events that track the creation and deletion of new VMs, particularly using non-standard ISO images and Operating Systems. Audit events may also record the threat actor downloading archived ISO images to the datastore volumes used by vSphere. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eHardening Guidance\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eIt is crucial to maintain an up-to-date inventory of appliances and other devices in the network that do not support the standard security tool stack. Any device in that inventory, whether internal or internet-facing, should be configured to follow a principle of least access.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eInternet access: Appliances should not have unrestricted access to the internet. Work with your vendors or monitor your firewall logs to lock down internet access to only those domains or IP addresses that the appliance requires to function properly.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eInternal network access: Appliances exposed to the internet should not have unrestricted access to internal IP address space. The management interface of most appliances does not need to establish connections to internal IP addresses. Work with the vendor to understand specific needsLDAP queries to verify user attributes for VPN logins.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant has \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/defending-vsphere-from-unc3944\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003epreviously published guidance\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e to secure the vSphere platform from threat actors. We recommend you follow the guidance, especially the forwarding of logs to a central SIEM, enabling vSphere lockdown mode, enforcing MFA for web logins, and enforcing the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eexecInstalledOnly\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e policy.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eOrganizations should assess and improve the isolation of any credential vaulting systems. In many cases if a threat actor is able to gain access to the underlying Operating System, any protected secrets can be exposed. Servers hosting credential vaulting applications should be considered Tier 0 systems and have strict access controls applied to them. Mandiant recommends organizations work with their vendors to adopt secure software practices such as storing encryption keys in the Trusted Platform Module (TPM) of the server.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eOutlook and Implications \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eRecent intrusion operations tied to BRICKSTORM likely represent an array of objectives ranging from geopolitical espionage, access operations, and intellectual property (IP) theft to enable exploit development. Based on evidence from recent investigations the targeting of the US legal space is primarily to gather information related to US national security and international trade. Additionally, GTIG assesses with high confidence that the objective of BRICKSTORM targeting SaaS providers is to gain access to downstream customer environments or the data SaaS providers host on their customers' behalf. The targeting of technology companies presents an opportunity to conduct theft of valuable IP to further the development of zero-day exploits. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAcknowledgements \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThis analysis would not have been possible without the assistance from across Google Threat Intelligence Group, Mandiant Consulting and FLARE. We would like to specifically thank Nick Simonian from GTIG Research and Discovery (RAD). We would also like to thank Ryan Tomcik from Mandiant Threat Defense (MTD) for contributing network detection content\u003c/span\u003e. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eIndicators of Compromise\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe following indicators of compromise are available in a \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.virustotal.com/gui/collection/cedb89304a0a11fc60ebe24cb7f3f42683d19132851e3e97199860359fe4d26c/summary\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eGoogle Threat Intelligence (GTI) collection\u003c/a\u003e. Note that Mandiant has not observed instances where the threat actor reused a malware sample and hunting for the exact indicators is unlikely to yield results.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cdiv align\u003d\"left\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv
